left: 0.741em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-36"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-37" style="font-size: 70.7%; font-family: STIXGeneral-Regular;">N</span><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-38" style="font-size: 70.7%; font-family: STIXGeneral-Regular;">a</span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 3.98em;"></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 2.219em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; vertical-align: -0.247em; border-left: 0px solid; width: 0px; height: 1.066em;"></span></span></nobr><span class="MJX_Assistive_MathML MJX_Assistive_MathML_Block" role="presentation"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" display="block" indentalign="left"><mrow><mrow><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><msub><mrow><mi>E</mi></mrow><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">a</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">d</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">s</mi></mrow></msub></mstyle></mrow></mrow><mrow><mo linebreak="nobreak">=</mo><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><msub><mrow><mi>E</mi></mrow><mrow><mtext mathvariant="normal">Surface</mtext><mo>+</mo><mi mathvariant="normal">N</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">a</mi></mrow></msub><mo>−</mo><msub><mrow><mi>E</mi></mrow><mrow><mtext mathvariant="normal">Surface</mtext></mrow></msub><mo>−</mo><msub><mrow><mi>E</mi></mrow><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">N</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">a</mi></mrow></msub></mstyle></mrow></math></span></span></div><script type="math/mml" id="MathJax-Element-1"><math display="block" indentalign="left"><mrow><mrow><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><msub><mrow><mi>E</mi></mrow><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">a</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">d</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">s</mi></mrow></msub></mstyle></mrow></mrow><mrow><mo linebreak="nobreak">=</mo><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><msub><mrow><mi>E</mi></mrow><mrow><mtext mathvariant="normal">Surface</mtext><mo>+</mo><mi mathvariant="normal">N</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">a</mi></mrow></msub><mo>−</mo><msub><mrow><mi>E</mi></mrow><mrow><mtext mathvariant="normal">Surface</mtext></mrow></msub><mo>−</mo><msub><mrow><mi>E</mi></mrow><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">N</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">a</mi></mrow></msub></mstyle></mrow></math></script></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(1)</span></div></div>where <i>E</i><sub>surface+Na</sub> and <i>E</i><sub>surface</sub> are the total energies of SnSb(100) surface with and without Na adsorbed, <i>E</i><sub>Na</sub> is the energy of per Na atom in the bulk metal. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>b and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.4c03240/suppl_file/ic4c03240_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S1</a>, Na cations can effectively adsorb onto sites a-e on the SnSb(100) surface, with <i>E</i><sub>ads</sub> values being negative, indicating that these positions are thermodynamically stable adsorption sites. The binding affinity of Sb atoms is slightly stronger than that of Sn atoms (−0.18 eV vs −0.10 eV), while the E site, characterized by multiple Sn/Sb–Na interactions, exhibits a binding affinity of −0.49 eV. Subsequently, we employed the Nudged Elastic Band (NEB) method to calculate the migration dynamics of Na from the most stable site E to neighboring positions (paths 1, 2, 4) or into the SnSb(100) surface (path 3). The migration paths are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>c-f, and the energy distribution is depicted in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>g. Path 4 has a double-peak topology with a local minimum in the middle, whereas other Na diffusion paths on the SnSb(100) surface display single-peak topologies. The energy barriers for paths 1 and 2 are less than 0.3 eV, indicating favorable charging rate performance. Due to the higher diffusion barrier of path 4, it is an unfavorable Na migration path on the SnSb(100) surface. Inserting surface-adsorbed Na into the SnSb crystal requires overcoming a barrier of up to 1.6 eV, indicating that this process is thermodynamically unfavorable and likely to produce unstable products. This migration pathway facilitates the understanding of sodium ion transport mechanisms and their distribution within the electrode, which is valuable for elucidating the kinetics of the sodiation/desodiation reactions. These results suggest that SnSb/C nanoparticles have significant potential for application in sodium-ion batteries. The low energy barriers of Na diffusion paths support good performance at high charge–discharge rates, and the material’s stable Na adsorption capacity further enhances battery cycle life and stability.</div><div class="NLM_p">The micromorphology of SnSb/C nanoparticles is shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>a-c. The SEM images clearly demonstrate the uniform distribution of SnSb alloy nanoparticles in the carbon matrix, while the mapping images of SnSb/C in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.4c03240/suppl_file/ic4c03240_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2a-d</a> further confirm this observation. Additionally, the SnSb alloy nanoparticles have a diameter of approximately 100 nm, indicating successful nanostructuring and integration with carbon. This nanocomposite structure improves both the rapid migration of sodium ions and the structural stability of the material. In comparison with the SEM images of material Sb/C provided in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.4c03240/suppl_file/ic4c03240_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S3(a-c)</a>, analysis reveals that the diameter of nanoparticles in Sb/C is approximately 25 nm, indicating smaller particles. This could lead to aggregation during charging and discharging processes due to the small size of the particles. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref38'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a> However, the presence of carbon provides some mitigation to its cycling stability. The SEM images presented in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.4c03240/suppl_file/ic4c03240_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S3(d-f)</a> illustrate the morphology of Sn/C, revealing particles with a size exceeding the micrometer scale. In contrast to the homogeneous incorporation of nanosized SnSb/C particles within the carbonaceous substrate, the distribution of Sn particles within Sn/C appears irregular, resulting in diminished sodium storage capabilities. To further investigate the internal structure of SnSb/C nanoparticles, TEM images of SnSb/C are depicted in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>d. Through the carbonaceous substrate, numerous SnSb alloy nanoparticles are observed, consistent with the SEM analysis, which adequately demonstrates the successful composite formation of SnSb alloy nanoparticles with carbon. The successful incorporation of the carbonaceous substrate not only enhances the conductivity of sodium storage performance but also alleviates the severe volume effects caused by alloy particles during sodium storage, thereby contributing to the enhancement of electrochemical performance. Furthermore, upon local magnification of the image in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>d, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>e, the diameter of SnSb alloy nanoparticles is clearly observed to be consistent with the results from SEM images, indicating uniformity in synthesized nanoparticle size. In <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>f, high-resolution lattice fringes of SnSb/C alloy nanoparticles are depicted, with lattice spacing determined to be 0.31 nm, matching the (101) crystal plane of SnSb alloy, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref39'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39" aria-label="reference 39">(39)</a> thereby affirming the successful preparation of SnSb/C. The selected area electron diffraction (SAED) pattern of SnSb/C is illustrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>g, displaying two concentric diffraction rings, with the yellow-marked and purple-marked diffraction rings corresponding to the (101) and (012) crystal planes of SnSb/C nanoparticles, respectively. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref40'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40" aria-label="reference 40">(40)</a> Consistent with the lattice fringes, this further confirms the formation of SnSb alloy, where synergistic effects between binary metals play a crucial role in enhancing sodium storage performance. To accurately observe the distribution of elements in the material, elemental mapping experiments were conducted. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>h-l, the results demonstrate the uniform distribution of Sn, Sb, and C elements within the SnSb/C structure. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.4c03240/suppl_file/ic4c03240_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4a-f</a> depicts elemental mapping images of Cl-SnSb/C obtained via scanning electron microscopy (SEM), illustrating the uniform distribution of Cl throughout the material. Analysis in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>m-o using energy-dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (EDS) reveals that Cl constitutes 1.28% of the elemental composition. High-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM) analysis of Cl-SnSb/C (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.4c03240/suppl_file/ic4c03240_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5a-h</a>) shows Cl primarily located on the surface of SnSb alloy particles, with minor distribution on the carbon substrate. Local magnification of chlorine-ion-regulated SnSb alloy particles confirms the uniform distribution of Sn and Sb particles on individual SnSb alloy particles, validating the successful implementation of the composite strategy and effective Cl incorporation.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.4c03240/asset/images/medium/ic4c03240_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.4c03240/asset/images/large/ic4c03240_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. (a–c) SEM image of SnSb/C nanoparticles. (d, e) TEM images of SnSb/C nanoparticles. (f) HRTEM image of SnSb/C nanoparticles. (g) SAED image of SnSb/C nanoparticles. (h–l)TEM element mapping images of (i) SnSb/C, (j) C, (k) Sn, (l) Sb. (m) EDS images of Cl-SnSb/C. (n) Table of distribution of element content. (o) Map of element species distribution.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.4c03240/asset/images/large/ic4c03240_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.inorgchem.4c03240&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.4c03240/asset/images/medium/ic4c03240_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.4c03240/asset/images/large/ic4c03240_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. (a) XRD images of SnSb/C, Sn/C and Sb/C. (b) Raman spectra of SnSb/C, Sn/C and Sb/C. (c) TGA curves of SnSb/C composites. (d) Illustration of N<sub>2</sub> adsorption–desorption isotherms and pore distribution of SnSb/C. XPS spectra including (e) full spectra, (f) C 1s, (g) Sn 3d, (h) Sb 3d, and (i) Cl 2p of Cl-SnSb/C.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.4c03240/asset/images/large/ic4c03240_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.inorgchem.4c03240&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The crystal compositions of SnSb/C, Sn/C, and Sb/C are illustrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>a. As depicted, the diffraction peaks of three kinds of materials correspond to the peaks of the standard cards for SnSb (JCPDS No. 33–0118), Sn (JCPDS No. 04–0673), and Sb (JCPDS No. 35–0572), respectively, indicating the successful synthesis of the three materials. The absence of impurity peaks and the high peak intensities in the Figure indicate that the prepared materials possess high phase purity and good crystallinity, consistent with the results analyzed from the transmission electron microscopy images. The diffraction patterns of Sb and Sn correspond to the <i>R</i><span class="overline">3</span><i>m</i>(166) and I41/amd (141) space groups, indicating hexagonal and tetragonal crystal phases, respectively. Specifically, the representative XRD pattern of SnSb nanoparticles embedded in SnSb/C composite material is shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.4c03240/suppl_file/ic4c03240_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a>, and the XRD pattern of SnSb/C can be indexed to a rhombohedral crystal phase with the <i>R</i><span class="overline">3</span><i>m</i>(166) space group. The structure of SnSb/C was refined using the GSAS program, and peak intensities indicate the formation of SnSb phase and good crystallinity in the composite material. Matching yielded lattice parameters of <i>a</i> = <i>b</i> = 4.32464 Å and <i>c</i> = 5.34284 Å, which agree well with literature values. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref41'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41" aria-label="reference 41">(41)</a> For a deeper investigation into the presence and defects of carbon, Raman characterization of three materials was conducted, as depicted in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>b. The figure illustrates a <i>D</i> peak at 1350 cm<sup>–1</sup> and a <i>G</i> peak at 1579 cm<sup>–1</sup>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref42'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42" aria-label="reference 42">(42)</a> The <i>D</i> band signifies disordered carbon, while the <i>G</i> band indicates ordered graphite carbon, providing evidence for the presence of carbon in the material. The <i>I</i><sub>D</sub><i>/I</i><sub>G</sub> values were simultaneously analyzed and calculated, yielding a value of 1.04 for SnSb/C, which is higher than those of Sn/C (1.00) and Sb/C (1.01). This further elucidates the presence of certain defects in the carbon of bimetallic SnSb/C nanoparticles, which can provide additional active sites, facilitating more Na<sup>+</sup> insertion and consequently enhancing sodium storage capacity. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref43'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43" aria-label="reference 43">(43)</a> To further explore the pyrolysis process of SnSb/C nanoparticles and estimate the carbon content in the material, we conducted thermogravimetric analysis (TGA) on SnSb/C, as illustrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>c. From the graph, it can be observed that there is a slight decrease in the curve around 100 °C, primarily due to the evaporation of moisture from the material. After reaching 420 °C, the curve exhibits a pronounced decrease until it reaches stability around 600 °C. This phenomenon can be attributed to two primary factors. First, the carbon within the material undergoes oxidation, forming carbon dioxide, which is released. Second, the oxidation of SnSb occurs, leading to the formation of tin dioxide and antimony tetroxide. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref44'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref44" aria-label="reference 44">(44)</a> Additionally, based on the curve, the carbon content in the material was calculated to be 39.6%. The existence of carbon is advantageous in mitigating structural fracture in the SnSb alloy during charge–discharge cycles, thus improving cycling stability. The TGA of Sn/C and Sb/C is depicted in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.4c03240/suppl_file/ic4c03240_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7a-b</a>. The observed curves indicate that the carbon content in the Sn/C sample is 28.4%, while that in the Sb/C sample is 23.8%. Additionally, we conducted a detailed investigation into the specific surface area and pore size distribution of three samples. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 3</a>d and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.4c03240/suppl_file/ic4c03240_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S8a-b</a> illustrate the N<sub>2</sub> adsorption–desorption isotherms of SnSb/C, Sn/C, and Sb/C. Their specific surface areas are 184.0 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>, 372.8 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>, and 180.6 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. According to the N<sub>2</sub> adsorption–desorption isotherms, the isotherm of SnSb/C exhibits a type IV curve with hysteresis, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref45'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref45" aria-label="reference 45">(45)</a> indicating a larger specific surface area, which is conducive to accelerating the migration of electrons/ions during cycling processes, thereby improving reaction kinetics. The excessively high specific surface area of Sn/C may be attributed to the lower content of elemental tin in the material, thereby exposing more carbon pores and yielding larger values. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref46'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref46" aria-label="reference 46">(46)</a> Additionally, from the pore size distribution curve in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>e, it can be observed that the pore sizes of all three samples are below 2 nm, indicating a microporous structure. The surface valence composition and chemical structure of Cl-SnSb/C have been studied by X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS). <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>e illustrates the XPS survey spectra of Cl-SnSb/C, compared with SnSb/C in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.4c03240/suppl_file/ic4c03240_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9</a>, revealing evident peaks corresponding to C 1s, O 1s, Sn 3d, and Sb 3d for both samples, indicating a similar composition of the surface elements. The distinguishing feature is the lower chlorine peak observed at 198.0 eV in Cl-SnSb/C, consistent with the results obtained from EDS analysis, confirming successful incorporation of chlorine ions into the sample. Meanwhile, narrow spectrum analysis was conducted for each element. The narrow spectrum of C 1s is shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>f. The fitting reveals a peak at 284.6 eV that corresponds to the C–C bond and a characteristic peak at 285.7 eV that corresponds to the C–O bond. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref47'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref47" aria-label="reference 47">(47)</a> The narrow spectrum of Sn 3d is displayed in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>g, showing clearly four distinct peaks obtained through fitting. Peaks located at 485.3 and 487.2 eV correspond to the Sn<sup>0</sup> 3d<sub>5/2</sub> and Sn<sup>4+</sup> 3d<sub>5/2</sub> states, respectively, while peaks at 493.9 and 495.7 eV match with the Sn<sup>0</sup> 3d<sub>3/2</sub> and Sn<sup>4+</sup> 3d<sub>3/2</sub> states. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref48'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref48" aria-label="reference 48">(48)</a> <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>h displays the fine spectrum of Sb 3d, revealing four characteristic peaks through peak fitting. The peak at 528.1 eV corresponds to the Sb<sup>0</sup> 3d<sub>5/2</sub> state, while the peak at 531.1 eV corresponds to the Sb<sup>3+</sup> 3d<sub>5/2</sub> state. Additionally, the peaks at 537.6 and 540.3 eV are attributed to the Sb<sup>0</sup> and Sb<sup>3+</sup> 3d<sub>3/2</sub> states, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref49'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref49" aria-label="reference 49">(49)</a> respectively. It is worth noting that both Sb and Sn narrow spectra exhibit peaks corresponding to both +3 and +4 oxidation states, primarily due to surface oxidation of the material. However, the presence of peaks corresponding to the 0 oxidation state indicates that the material is not fully oxidized. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref50'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref50" aria-label="reference 50">(50)</a> <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>i presents a high-resolution spectrum of chlorine, where distinct peaks at Cl 2p<sub>1/2</sub> and Cl 2p<sub>3/2</sub>, located at 200.0 and 198.0 eV respectively, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref51'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref51" aria-label="reference 51">(51)</a> indicate significant enrichment of chlorine within both the surface and internal regions of the material.</div><div class="NLM_p">We assembled a series of CR2025 coin-type half-cells inside a glovebox filled with argon gas to investigate the electrochemical performance of Cl-SnSb/C, SnSb/C, Sn/C, and Sb/C. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>a shows the cyclic voltammetry (CV) test of Cl-SnSb/C, which was conducted to analyze its reaction mechanism. The CV test was performed at a scan rate of 0.2 mV s<sup>–1</sup> over a voltage range of 0–3 V. During the beginning cycle of the cathode scan, a small peak was observed, appearing at 1.12 V, indicating that chloride ions preferentially reacted to form sodium chloride (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.4c03240/suppl_file/ic4c03240_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10</a>). A wide and distinct peak appeared within the voltage range of 0.5 V-0.1 V, attributed to the degradation of the electrolyte, progressively leading to the formation of the SEI film. With an increase in the number of cycles, the SEI film evolved into a new stage, which improved the interface stability by inhibiting electrolyte side reactions during the process of sodium ion stripping. Additionally, in the initial cycle, the observed cathodic peaks were notably distinct in position from those of the following cycles, primarily due to alloying reactions and the SEI film formation during the first cycle. During the second cathodic scanning process, the observed two reduction peaks were ascribed to the multistep alloying reactions of Cl-SnSb/C. Studies have shown that Sn has a lower reaction potential than Sb. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref52 ref53'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref52 ref53" aria-label="reference 52 and 53">(52,53)</a> Therefore, the alloying of Na<sup>+</sup> with Sb occurs at 0.57 V, where Na atoms replace Sb atoms to form Na<sub>3</sub>Sb (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq2" aria-label="reaction 2">reaction 2</a>). At 0.21 V, Na<sup>+</sup> reacts with Sn to form Na<sub>15</sub>Sn<sub>4</sub> (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq3" aria-label="reaction 3">reaction 3</a>). In this process, Sn acts as a buffer, reducing internal stress and mitigating the volume expansion effect to some extent, which is an advantage of the SnSb alloy. Furthermore, the CV curve of Sn in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.4c03240/suppl_file/ic4c03240_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S11a</a> indicates that during the anodic scan of the second cycle, the multistep dealloying reactions of Na<sub>3</sub>Sn (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq4" aria-label="reaction 4">reaction 4</a>) and Na<sub>15</sub>Sn<sub>4</sub> (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq5" aria-label="reaction 5">reaction 5</a>) occur at 0.19 and 0.63 V, respectively. The oxidation peaks at 0.78 and 0.93 V are due to the dealloying of Na<sub>3</sub>Sb (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq6" aria-label="reaction 6">reaction 6</a>). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref53 ref54 ref55'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref53 ref54 ref55" aria-label="reference 53 to 55">(53−55)</a> Importantly, the cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves from multiple scans show good overlap, indicating the excellent reversibility of the chlorine-ion-regulated bimetallic Cl-SnSb/C nanoparticles. The exact processes of the reaction are as described below:</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.4c03240/asset/images/medium/ic4c03240_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.4c03240/asset/images/large/ic4c03240_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. (i) Plot of Z’ versus ω<sup>–1/2</sup> in the low-frequency range for Cl-SnSb/C, Sb/C, and Sn/C after 3 cycles.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div -999.997em); top: -3.974em; left: 0em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-155"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-156" style="font-family: STIXGeneral-Regular;">xNa</span></span><span style="display: class="MathJax_Preview" style="color: inherit;"></span><div class="MathJax_Display" style="text-align: left;"><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-6-Frame" tabindex="0" data-mathml="<math STIXGeneral-Regular; padding-left: 0.344em;">→</span><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-182" style="font-family: STIXGeneral-Regular; padding-left: 0.344em;">Sb</span><span class="mo" id="MathJax-Span-183" style="font-family: STIXGeneral-Regular; padding-left: 0.23em;">+</span><span class="msup" id="MathJax-Span-184" style="padding-left: 0.23em;"><span style="display: inline-block; id="MathJax-Span-185"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-186" style="font-family: STIXGeneral-Regular;">xNa</span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: width: 1.48em; height: 0px;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(3.355em, 1000.91em, 4.151em, -999.997em); top: -3.974em; left: 0em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-191"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-192" style="font-family: STIXGeneral-Regular;">xe</span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 3.98em;"></span></span><span style="position: absolute; top: -4.372em; left: 0.969em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-193"><span class="mo" id="MathJax-Span-194" style="font-size: 70.7%; font-family: STIXGeneral-Regular;">−</span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 3.98em;"></span></span></span></span><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-195"><span class="mo" id="MathJax-Span-196" style="font-family: STIXGeneral-Regular;">(</span><span class="mn" id="MathJax-Span-197" style="font-family: STIXGeneral-Regular;">1</span><span class="mo" id="MathJax-Span-198" style="font-family: STIXGeneral-Regular; padding-left: 0.344em;">&lt;</span><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-199" style="font-family: STIXGeneral-Regular; padding-left: 0.344em;">x</span><span class="mo" id="MathJax-Span-200" style="font-family: STIXGeneral-Regular; padding-left: 0.344em;">&lt;</span><span class="mn" id="MathJax-Span-201" style="font-family: STIXGeneral-Regular; padding-left: 0.344em;">3</span><span class="mo" id="MathJax-Span-202" style="font-family: STIXGeneral-Regular;">)</span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 2.219em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; vertical-align: -0.247em; border-left: 0px solid; width: 0px; height: 1.191em;"></span></span></nobr><span class="MJX_Assistive_MathML MJX_Assistive_MathML_Block" role="presentation"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" display="block" indentalign="left" indentshift="3.0em" indentshiftfirst="0em" linebreak="auto"><msub><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">Na</mi></mrow><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">x</mi></mrow></msub><mi mathvariant="normal">Sb</mi><mo>→</mo><mi mathvariant="normal">Sb</mi><mo>+</mo><msup><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">xNa</mi></mrow><mrow><mo>+</mo></mrow></msup><mo>+</mo><msup><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">xe</mi></mrow><mrow><mo>−</mo></mrow></msup><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mn>1</mn><mo>&lt;</mo><mi mathvariant="normal">x</mi><mo>&lt;</mo><mn>3</mn><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow></math></span></span></div><script type="math/mml" id="MathJax-Element-6"><math display="block" indentalign="left" indentshift="3.0em" indentshiftfirst="0em" linebreak="auto"><msub><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">Na</mi></mrow><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">x</mi></mrow></msub><mi mathvariant="normal">Sb</mi><mo>→</mo><mi mathvariant="normal">Sb</mi><mo>+</mo><msup><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">xNa</mi></mrow><mrow><mo>+</mo></mrow></msup><mo>+</mo><msup><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">xe</mi></mrow><mrow><mo>−</mo></mrow></msup><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mn>1</mn><mo>&lt;</mo><mi mathvariant="normal">x</mi><mo>&lt;</mo><mn>3</mn><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow></math></script></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(6)</span></div></div></div><div class="NLM_p">In addition, the CV curves of Sn/C and Sb/C under the same experimental setup are presented in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.4c03240/suppl_file/ic4c03240_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S11a,c</a>. It can be observed that the position of the peak in the CV curve of the initial circle is obviously not the same as from that of the following cycle, which is obviously influenced by SEI. In the process of anode scanning in the first circle, Sn/C has multiple continuous oxidation peaks, respectively at 0.24 V, 0.58 and 0.69 V, which is a multistep dealloying reaction with Sn. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref56'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref56" aria-label="reference 56">(56)</a> The CV curve of Sb/C is shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.4c03240/suppl_file/ic4c03240_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S11c</a>, and its first circle is also affected by SEI. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>b presents the electrostatic charge/discharge (GCD) curves of the SnSb/C anode under different cycles at 0.1 Ag<sup>1–</sup>. As observed in the figure, the SnSb/C electrode exhibits a charging plateau at 0.57 V and discharging plateaus at 0.45 V, which correspond approximately to the redox peaks in the CV curve, and the charge–discharge curves also show good overlap, indicating excellent cycling stability. The initial charging capacity of SnSb/C is 573.0 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, while the initial discharging capacity is 860.5 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, with an initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE) of 66.6%. Notably, the ICE increases to 89.3% from the second cycle onward and continues to improve. This enhancement mainly arises from the development of the SEI film on the electrode interface during the initial cycle. This film acts as a protective barrier, shielding the electrode from interacting with the electrolyte and diminishing interfacial side reactions. To compare, the GCD curves of Sn/C and Sb/C under identical conditions are illustrated in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.4c03240/suppl_file/ic4c03240_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S11b,d</a>. It is evident that the SnSb/C electrode (860.5 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>) has a higher specific discharge capacity than the Sn/C (625.3 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>) and Sb/C (454.8 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>) electrodes. Additionally, SnSb/C exhibits a higher ICE compared to Sn/C (56.8%) and Sb/C (65.2%). The above results demonstrate that the composite SnSb/C alloy nanoparticles exhibit significant electrochemical performance.</div><div class="NLM_p">To further boost the performance of the electrode material, chloride ion regulation was implemented. The cycling stability of Cl-SnSb/C, SnSb/C, Sn/C, and Sb/C at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup> is depicted in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>c. The first charge and discharge capacities of Cl-SnSb/C are 762.4 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> and 1023.5 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively, with a first-cycle Coulombic efficiency (CE) of 74.5%. For SnSb/C, the first charge and discharge capacities are 550.7 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> and 815.1 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, with a CE of 67.6%. In comparison, the CE of Sb/C and Sn/C are 65.2% and 56.8%, respectively. The first-cycle Coulombic efficiency reflects the influence of the SEI on the material, indicating whether it has a good capacity retention rate. The above data indicate that the SEI film formed in the first cycle of Cl-SnSb/C with chloride ion regulation is relatively stable, resulting in less irreversible capacity loss. Although SnSb/C is slightly inferior to Cl-SnSb/C, it still outperforms pure Sb/C and Sn/C. Furthermore, it is evident from the figure that Cl-SnSb/C maintains a capacity of 500.4 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> after 100 cycles at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>, whereas SnSb/C, Sb/C, and Sn/C degrade to 375.7 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, 230.4 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, and 71.5 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively, under the same conditions. This demonstrates that Cl-SnSb/C not only has excellent specific capacity but also superior stability. The chloride ion regulation improves the Coulombic efficiency in the initial cycle and maintains high electrochemical performance over long-term use, reducing capacity decay. Additionally, the comparison of SnSb/C, Sb/C, and Sn/C data shows that the bimetallic alloy effectively buffers volume expansion and reduces structural stress, while the presence of carbon contributes to the reversibility during cycling. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref57'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref57" aria-label="reference 57">(57)</a> <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>d illustrates the rate performance of all materials. At a current density of 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>, Cl-SnSb/C exhibits a specific discharge capacity of 761.7 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>. When the current density increases to 0.2, 0.4, 0.8, and 1.6 (unit: A g<sup>–1</sup>), the specific discharge capacities of Cl-SnSb/C are 614.8, 539.5, 466, and 394.4 (unit: mAh g<sup>–1</sup>), respectively. When the current density is returned to 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>, the discharge capacity of Cl-SnSb/C recovers to 702 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> after 50 cycles at each rate. It is noteworthy that after the following 30 cycles, it remains at 676.8 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, indicating significant rate capability. In comparison, SnSb/C exhibits a specific discharge capacity of 555 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>, and after a series of high-rate cycles, its capacity recovers to 488 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> when the current density is reverted to 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>. This indicates that Cl-SnSb/C possesses good ion and electron transport properties during rapid charge–discharge processes, effectively adapting to changes in current density, further demonstrating that the cycling stability and reversibility of Cl-SnSb/C are superior to those of SnSb/C. When Sn/C and Sb/C are cycled at high rates and restored to 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>, their discharge specific capacities recover only to 102.5 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> and 286.2 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. This indicates that bimetallic SnSb/C nanoparticles exhibit better rate performance compared to monometallic counterparts, highlighting the effectiveness of the bimetallic alloy and carbon-based design strategy. These data demonstrate that Cl-SnSb/C not only exhibits excellent cycling stability but also shows good rate performance, indicating significant enhancements in structural optimization, conductivity, and ion transport properties of the material. This confirms the success of the chloride ion control and carbon-based bimetallic alloy design strategy, providing an effective solution for the development of high-performance anode materials. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref58'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref58" aria-label="reference 58">(58)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">Based on these findings, we further focused on the anode materials with the best performance: Cl-SnSb/C, Sn/C, and Sb/C. To investigate and assess the impact of Na<sup>+</sup> transfer rate and charge displacement characteristics on electrochemical performance, we show the EIS tests for Cl-SnSb/C, Sn/C and Sb/C in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>e. Clearly, the Nyquist plot is composed of a semicircle in the high-frequency range and a sloping line in the low-frequency range. This plot can be analyzed in two parts: the semicircle portion and the low-frequency sloping portion. The <i>z</i>-axis intercept in the high-frequency region represents the ohmic resistance (<i>R</i><sub>e</sub>) of the electrode, electrolyte and separator. The high-frequency semicircle corresponds to the resistance of Na<sup>+</sup> passing through the SEI film (<i>R</i><sub>SEI</sub>), indicating Na<sup>+</sup> transport within this frequency range. The semicircle in the midfrequency range represents the transition resistance and charge transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>), while the Warburg impedance (<i>Z</i><sub>w</sub>), related to Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion within the samples, is associated with the slope in the low-frequency region. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref59'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref59" aria-label="reference 59">(59)</a> In the EIS plots, a larger charge transfer resistance corresponds to a larger diameter of the midfrequency semicircle. The resistance to Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion decreases as the slope of the low-frequency region increases; a higher slope indicates lower resistance. By comparing the impedance spectra of the three materials in their fresh states and after three cycles, it is evident that the diameter of the semicircle for Cl-SnSb/C is smaller than that for Sn/C and Sb/C, both in the fresh state and after three cycles. Additionally, the slope of the straight line is relatively larger, indicating lower charge transfer resistance and faster diffusion, which is beneficial for rapid ion transport and enhanced conductivity. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref60'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref60" aria-label="reference 60">(60)</a> This suggests that the electronegativity of chloride ions alters the nearby environment of the ion-conducting inorganic salts, potentially promoting the formation of a more uniform SEI layer. A uniform SEI layer effectively covers the electrode surface, reducing interfacial inhomogeneities and local reaction activity, thereby lowering the <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>. Moreover, a stable SEI layer mitigates the impact of volume changes in the electrode material during charge and discharge cycles on ion diffusion, enabling faster insertion and extraction of sodium ions from the electrode material. To further accurately analyze the impedance performance of Cl-SnSb/C, we conducted circuit simulations, as shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.4c03240/suppl_file/ic4c03240_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S12</a>. The fitted impedance values for the three materials in their fresh condition and after three cycles are presented in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>f,g. By comparison of the <i>R</i><sub>e</sub> numbers in the figures, it is evident that the electrolyte resistance of Cl-SnSb/C is lower both in the fresh state and after three cycles. This indicates that the uniform SEI layer reduces the complexity of reactions at the electrolyte-electrode interface, resulting in lower electrolyte resistance and enhanced sodium ion transport efficiency. Furthermore, the <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> value for fresh Cl-SnSb/C is 29.4 Ω, which is lower than that of Sn/C (44.9 Ω) and Sb/C (57.7 Ω). Similarly, after three cycles, Cl-SnSb/C exhibits a significantly lower <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> value (41.9 Ω) compared to Sn/C (92.5 Ω) and Sb/C (119.9 Ω). These data demonstrate that the Cl ion-regulated bimetallic Cl-SnSb/C nanocomposite enhances ion/electron transfer, improves material conductivity, and offers a distinct advantage in kinetic reactions. In addition, in order to better analyze the diffusion process of Na<sup>+</sup>, the following equation shows the relationship between the slope of the fitting line between <i>Z’</i> and ω<sup>–1/2</sup> and the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient (<i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub>):<div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq7"><div class="equation"><span class="MathJax_Preview" style="color: inherit;"></span><div class="MathJax_Display" style="text-align: left;"><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-7-Frame" tabindex="0" data-mathml="<math xmlns=&quot;http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML&quot; display=&quot;block&quot; indentalign=&quot;left&quot;><mrow><mrow><mstyle indentshift=&quot;3em&quot; indentshiftlast=&quot;3em&quot;><mi mathvariant=&quot;italic&quot;>D</mi></mstyle></mrow></mrow><mrow><mo linebreak=&quot;nobreak&quot;>=</mo><mstyle indentshift=&quot;3em&quot; indentshiftlast=&quot;3em&quot;><mfrac><mrow><msup><mrow><mi>R</mi></mrow><mrow><mn>2</mn></mrow></msup><msup><mrow><mi>T</mi></mrow><mrow><mn>2</mn></mrow></msup></mrow><mrow><mn>2</mn><msup><mrow><mi>A</mi></mrow><mrow><mn>2</mn></mrow></msup><msup><mrow><mi>n</mi></mrow><mrow><mn>4</mn></mrow></msup><msup><mrow><mi>F</mi></mrow><mrow><mn>4</mn></mrow></msup><msup><mrow><mi>C</mi></mrow><mrow><mn>2</mn></mrow></msup><msup><mrow><msub><mrow><mi>&amp;#x3C3;</mi></mrow><mrow><mi>&amp;#x3C9;</mi></mrow></msub></mrow><mrow><mn>2</mn></mrow></msup></mrow></mfrac></mstyle></mrow></math>" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" display="block" indentalign="left"><mrow><mrow><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><msup><mrow><mi>Z</mi></mrow><mrow><mo class="MJX-variant" mathsize="8pt">′</mo></mrow></msup></mstyle></mrow></mrow><mrow><mo linebreak="nobreak">=</mo><mstyle indentshift="3em" type="math/mml" id="MathJax-Element-8"><math display="block" indentalign="left"><mrow><mrow><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><msup><mrow><mi>Z</mi></mrow><mrow><mo class="MJX-variant" mathsize="8pt">′</mo></mrow></msup></mstyle></mrow></mrow><mrow><mo linebreak="nobreak">=</mo><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><msub><mrow><mi>R</mi></mrow><mrow><mi>e</mi></mrow></msub><mo>+</mo><msub><mrow><mi>R</mi></mrow><mrow><mi>S</mi><mi>E</mi><mi>I</mi></mrow></msub><mo>+</mo><msub><mrow><mi>R</mi></mrow><mrow><mi>C</mi><mi>T</mi></mrow></msub><mo>+</mo><mi>σ</mi><msup><mrow><mi>ω</mi></mrow><mrow><mo>−</mo><mn>1</mn><mo>/</mo><mn>2</mn></mrow></msup></mstyle></mrow></math></script></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(8)</span></div></div></div><div class="NLM_p">As shown in the two equations above, <i>R</i> represents the gas constant, <i>T</i> represents the absolute temperature, <i>A</i> and <i>F</i> represent the electrode surface area and the Faraday constant, respectively, as well as <i>C</i> and n represent the concentration of sodium ions and the number of electrons per molecule in the oxidation process, respectively. The Warburg factor σ<sub>ω</sub> is obtained by fitting the relationship between <i>Z’</i> and ω<sup>–1/2</sup> (ω = 2<i>πf</i>). From this relation, it can be seen that the smaller the Warburg factor (σ<sub>ω</sub>), the better the value of <i>D</i><sub>Na<sup>+</sup></sub>, indicating the stronger the diffusion ability. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>h,i shows the σ<sub>ω</sub> values of the three samples when fresh state and after three cycles, respectively.</div><div class="NLM_p">From the figure, it can be observed that in the beginning state, the σ<sub>ω</sub> value of the Cl-SnSb/C electrode is 284.4 cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup>, whereas the σ<sub>ω</sub> values for the Sn/C and Sb/C electrodes are 490.3 cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup> and 996.5 cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. This indicates that a relatively stable and uniform SEI layer has already formed on the Cl-SnSb/C electrode at the onset of the charge–discharge cycles. After three charge–discharge cycles, the Cl-SnSb/C electrode still exhibits the smallest σ<sub>ω</sub> value (272.0 cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup>) among the three electrode materials in the <i>Z’</i> versus ω<sup>–1/2</sup> plots, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref61'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref61" aria-label="reference 61">(61)</a> suggesting that the SEI layer on the Cl-SnSb/C electrode has maintained good stability and integrity without significant degradation. The above findings, which are consistent with the results of electrochemical performance tests, indicate that the Cl-SnSb/C bimetallic nanoparticles controlled by chloride ions exhibit faster diffusion kinetics in sodium-ion batteries and have significant advantages in improving the kinetics of sodium storage reactions.</div><div class="NLM_p">To more understand the reaction kinetics and capacitive behavior of Cl-SnSb/C, The CV tests performed on Cl-SnSb/C at scan rates from 0.2 to 1.0 mV<sup>–1</sup> are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>a. As observed in the figure, the CV curves exhibit very similar oxidation/reduction peaks as the scan rate rised from 0.2 to 1.0 mV<sup>–1</sup>. Additionally, The positions of the oxidation and reduction peaks shift up and down, respectively, with increasing scan rate, while the number of peaks stays the same, which indicates consistent electrochemical reactions. The capacitive contribution behavior can be predicted using the following equation: <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref62'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref62" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.4c03240/asset/images/medium/ic4c03240_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.4c03240/asset/images/large/ic4c03240_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. (a) CV curve of Cl-SnSb/C electrodes at varioust scanning rates. (b) The <i>b</i> values for different oxidation and reduction states. (c) Contribution of capacitance control with a scan rate of 0.2 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. (d) Comparison of the contributions of Cl-SnSb/C, Sn/C and Sb/C to the capacitance control at different scan rates. (e) GITT voltage curves for Cl-SnSb/C, Sn/C and Sb/C in the second cycle. (f) <i>E</i> and <i>t</i> curves of Cl-SnSb/C electrode under a single GITT. (g, h) Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients of Cl-SnSb/C, Sn/C, and Sb/C during discharge and charging.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.4c03240/asset/images/large/ic4c03240_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.inorgchem.4c03240&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">In the above equation, <i>i</i> and <i>v</i> denote the peak current and scan rate, respectively, while <i>a</i> and <i>b</i> are constants, with <i>b</i> indicating the capacitive contribution. Typically, The storage behavior is attributed to a diffusion-controlled process (battery behavior) when <i>b</i> = 0.5. When <i>b</i> = 1, the storage behavior is attributed to a surface-controlled process (pseudocapacitive behavior). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref63'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref63" aria-label="reference 63">(63)</a> Based on the three peaks present in the CV curves, the fitted <i>b</i> values for the Cl-SnSb/C electrode at peaks 1, 2, and 3 are 0.72, 0.65, and 0.7, respectively, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>b. These values, all between 0.5 and 1, indicate that the charge–discharge process of Cl-SnSb/C is governed by both battery and pseudocapacitive behaviors. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.4c03240/suppl_file/ic4c03240_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S13a</a> shows the CV derivative plots for the comparative material Sn/C under the same conditions, with minimal changes in the positions of the redox peaks. The <i>b</i> values fitted for different redox peaks, as shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.4c03240/suppl_file/ic4c03240_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S13b</a>, are 0.7 and 0.89, indicating that the sodium storage process in Sn/C is also mixed-controlled, consistent with the main material. The CV curves for the comparative material Sb/C at various scan rates are shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.4c03240/suppl_file/ic4c03240_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S13b</a>. Similarly, the CV curves exhibit slight shifts with increasing scan rates, with minimal changes in peak positions. The <i>b</i> values, representing the capacitive contribution, are greater than 0.5 but less than 1, as shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.4c03240/suppl_file/ic4c03240_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S13e</a>. For Cl-SnSb/C, the <i>b</i> values are relatively smaller, indicating that the process is primarily diffusion-controlled at low scan rates and predominantly capacitive at high scan rates.</div><div class="NLM_p">Moreover, pseudocapacitive characteristics are commonly considered desirable for achieving better rate characteristics. Hence, to understand the pseudocapacitive capacity in the test current, the capacitive contribution rate for the bimetallic nanoparticles Cl-SnSb/C was further calculated using the following formula: <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref64'); For the Cl-SnSb/C electrode, at a scan rate of 0.2 mV s<sup>–1</sup>, the contribution of pseudocapacitive properties can reach 42.2%, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>c. Furthermore, the pseudocapacitive contributions of Sn/C and Sb/C, as shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.4c03240/suppl_file/ic4c03240_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S13c,f</a>, are 31.1% and 37.9%, respectively, both lower than that of Cl-SnSb/C. This indicates that chloride ions alter the chemical environment of the inorganic salts in the material, thereby enhancing charge storage capacity and pseudocapacitive behavior. We employed the same method to calculate the contributions of pseudocapacitive characteristics of Cl-SnSb/C, Sn/C, and Sb/C at various scan rates. As illustrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>d, by analyzing the pseudocapacitive contribution rates of the three materials, it is clear that as the scan rate increases, the capacitive contribution rate gradually increases and gradually becomes dominated by capacitive behavior. This is benificial for enhancing the rate behavior of the materials, accelerating reaction kinetics, and demonstrating excellent sodium storage performance. Furthermore, it can be observed from the figure that the contributions of pseudointercalation properties for Cl-SnSb/C at scan speeds of 0.2, 0.4, 0.6, 0.8, and 1.0 mV s<sup>–1</sup> are 42.2%, 48.1%, 52.9%, 57.9%, and 62.0%, respectively. These values, combined with the outstanding cycling and high rate behaviors of the Cl-SnSb/C composite, are considerably greater than the pseudocapacitive contributions of Sn/C and Sb/C. This indicates that the introduction of chloride ions promotes the uniform formation of the SEI (solid electrolyte interphase) membrane, reduces defects and cracks in the SEI membrane, thereby improving its stability and ion conductivity.</div><div class="NLM_p">In order to investigated the sodium reaction kinetics of Cl-SnSb/C, Sn/C and Sb/C electrodes, the galvanostatic intermittent titration technique(GITT) was employed. The GITT curves for Cl-SnSb/C, Sn/C, and Sb/C materials in the voltage range of 0.005 to 3.0 V are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>e. Additionally, the diffusion rates of Na<sup>+</sup> ions were calculated through single-step GITT tests, as depicted in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>f. The single-step testing process involves pulse, constant current, and relaxation phases. the previous formula, τ denotes the current pulse time (s), common m<sub>B</sub>, <i>V</i><sub>M</sub>, <i>M</i><sub>B</sub> and <i>S</i> are respectively the mass, molar volume, molar mass and contact area of the electrode and electrolyte solution, respectively, <i>ΔE</i>s is the potential variation after the discharging pulse and open static, <i>ΔE</i>τ is the potential variation during the discharging pulse. The Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients of Cl-SnSb/C, Sn/C and Sb/C were obtained by analyzing the size of, nanoparticle and the tested data, as indicated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>(g-h). The Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient of Cl-SnSb/C during discharge ranges from 1.21 × 10<sup>–14</sup> to 3.43 × 10<sup>–14</sup>, while that during charging ranges from 7.94 × 10<sup>–16</sup> to 5.37 × 10<sup>–15</sup>, both higher than Sn/C and Sb/C, indicating that Cl-SnSb/C can efficiently and rapidly undergo the insertion and extraction processes of sodium ions, with Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics being more rapid, particularly in the dealloying reaction.</div><div class="NLM_p">This paper presents an in situ electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) analysis of Cl-SnSb/C assembled half-cells. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>a, the voltage points selected during the discharge process were 3.0, 2.5, 2.0, 1.0, 0.8, 0.7, 0.4, and 0.1 V. During the charging process, the chosen voltage points were 0.1, 0.3, 0.6, 0.7, 1.4, 2.0, 2.5, and 3.0 V. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>b shows the Nyquist plots during discharge. The <i>R</i><sub>CT</sub>, which is approximatly the radius in the midfrequency region, exhibits an overall decreasing trend as discharge progresses. To account for the interplay of various physical and chemical reactions during charging and discharging, this investigation employed Distributed Resistance Theory (DRT) to further analyzed the data-mathml="<math xmlns=&quot;http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML&quot; display=&quot;block&quot; indentalign=&quot;left&quot;><mrow><mrow><mstyle indentshift=&quot;3em&quot; class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-573"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-574"><span class="mstyle" id="MathJax-Span-575"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-576"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-577" id="MathJax-Span-585"><span class="mn" id="MathJax-Span-586" style="font-family: STIXGeneral-Regular;">2</span><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-587" style="font-family: STIXGeneral-Italic;">𝜋<span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; height: 1px; width: 0.003em;"></span></span><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-588" style="font-family: STIXGeneral-Italic;">𝑓<span style="display: rect(0.855em, 1001.82em, 1.253em, -999.997em); top: -1.304em; left: 0em;"><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; vertical-align: 0em; border-top: 1.3px solid; width: 1.821em; height: class="MJX_Assistive_MathML MJX_Assistive_MathML_Block" role="presentation"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" display="block" indentalign="left"><mrow><mrow><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mi>τ</mi></mstyle></mrow></mrow><mrow><mo linebreak="nobreak">=</mo><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mfrac><mrow><mn>1</mn></mrow><mrow><mn>2</mn><mi>π</mi><mi>f</mi></mrow></mfrac></mstyle></mrow></math></span></span></div><script type="math/mml" id="MathJax-Element-16"><math display="block" indentalign="left"><mrow><mrow><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mi>τ</mi></mstyle></mrow></mrow><mrow><mo linebreak="nobreak">=</mo><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mfrac><mrow><mn>1</mn></mrow><mrow><mn>2</mn><mi>π</mi><mi>f</mi></mrow></mfrac></mstyle></mrow></math></script></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(16)</span></div></div></div><figure id="fig6" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.4c03240/asset/images/medium/ic4c03240_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.4c03240/asset/images/large/ic4c03240_0006.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. Discharge and charge state (a) selected voltage points, (b, e) Nyquist diagram, (c, f) DRT calculation results, (d, g) contour diagram of DRT calculation, (h, i) σ<sub>ω</sub> value, (j) Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion rate <i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.4c03240/asset/images/large/ic4c03240_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.inorgchem.4c03240&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">DRT is a method to regularize EIS data by computing a distribution function g<i>(τ)</i> for a characteristic time <i>(τ)</i>. Afterward, the internal kinetic information on the battery is derived based on the g<i>(τ)</i> analysis, as described in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq14" aria-label="Equation 14">Equation 14</a>. In this equation, <i>R</i><sub>e</sub> and <i>R</i><sub>pol</sub> represent the ohmic resistance and polarization resistance respectively, τ and g<i>(τ)</i> denote the relaxation time and its distribution function, <i>j</i> and ω are imaginary unit and angular frequency. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>c presents the DRT analysis of the impedance information for the Cl-SnSb/C assembled battery. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref67'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref67" aria-label="reference 67">(67)</a> Since the electrolyte does not participate in redox reactions, the ohmic resistance <i>R</i><sub>e</sub> remains constant before and after cycling. We may infer that the peak at Log<i>(τ)</i> = −5 corresponds to the ohmic resistance, which is primarily related to the resistance of the electrode materials, electrolyte, and separator. In <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>c, the ohmic resistances are 5.61, 4.84, 4.86, 4.50, 4.44, 4.06, 4.13, and 4.57, displaying a trend of initial decrease followed by an increase. This behavior can be attributed to the following factors: during the initial discharge stage (3.0 to 0.8 V), sodium ions begin intercalating into the anode material, forming the SEI film. As the discharge process progresses, the electrode material gradually becomes activated, and the interfacial contact resistance decreases, resulting in a reduction of the ohmic resistance. Additionally, as the SEI film forms, the interfacial stability improves, leading to a decrease in interfacial resistance and consequently, a lower overall ohmic resistance. However, during the lower voltage discharge stage (0.8 to 0.1 V), the continued growth of the SEI film consumes more sodium salts from the electrolyte, decreasing the ion concentration in the electrolyte. This reduction in sodium ion carriers leads to a decrease in conductivity, thereby increasing the ohmic resistance. During the process of discharging the battery for the first time, the SEI layer forms, resulting in a high initial SEI impedance. However, as the SEI layer becomes fully established, its impedance decreases. Hence, the peak at Log<i>(τ)</i> = −4 can be ascribed to the <i>R</i><sub>SEI</sub>. As illustrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>c, during the discharge process (3.0 V-0.1 V), the SEI resistances are 18.5, 15.1, 12.7, 13.8, 13.9, 12.2, 12.0, and 11.8, indicating an overall decreasing trend. The significant decrease in SEI resistance at the early stage of discharge (3.0 V-2.0 V) indicates that the interfacial reactions between the electrode material and the electrolyte are quite active, forming a relatively stable SEI layer and reducing the interfacial resistance. During the midstage of discharge (2.0 V-0.8 V), the SEI resistance exhibits some fluctuations, possibly caused by the volume changes of the electrode material and the continuous decomposition of the electrolyte, which may alter the SEI layer structure. However, the SEI resistance remains relatively low overall. In the late stage of discharge (0.7 V-0.1 V), the SEI resistance decreases to its lowest point, indicating that the SEI layer has reached a more stable state, further reducing interfacial resistance and facilitating sodium ion intercalation. According to Arrhenius equation, the activation energy of a chemical process is affected by temperature. The significant change that occurs at peaks between Log<i>(τ)</i> = −3 to −2 can be attributed to the <i>R</i><sub>CT</sub>. Due to the uncoupling of the impedance si, the peak at Log<i>(τ)</i> = −1 is considered to be noise.</div><div class="NLM_p">Using the DRT tool to investigate of the impedance information in the midfrequency range, it can be observed from <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>d that the R<sub>CT</sub> first increases and then gradually decreases as the depth of discharge increases. This is due to the initial reaction between the electrode material and the electrolyte, with sodium ions intercalating into the electrode structure. During this process, the surface and interfacial reactions of the electrode material are not yet fully stabilized, resulting in increased charge transfer resistance. As the SEI gradually forms and stabilizes, the charge transfer process becomes smoother, reducing interfacial resistance. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>e shows the Nyquist plots during the charging procedure. The observed decrease in ohmic resistance during the latter stages of charging could be ascribed to the restabilization of the electrode and SEI layer, as well as the equilibrium stabilization of ion concentration in the electrolyte, leading to a reduction in interfacial resistance. Following DRT calculations on <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>e, <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>f is obtained, and the contour plot is presented in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>g. From <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>g, it can be seen that <i>R</i><sub>CT</sub> is relatively low at both high and low voltage states, indicating lower interfacial resistance and a more efficient electrochemical reaction. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>h shows the σ<sub>ω</sub> values during the charging state, which are 13, 9, 17, 25, 519, 1257, 1343, and 1504, respectively. The initially low σ<sub>ω</sub> values indicate that the diffusion resistance of sodium ions within the electrode material is relatively low. As the voltage increases and sodium ions are further extracted, the diffusion pathways become obstructed, leading to increased diffusion resistance. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>i presents the σ<sub>ω</sub> values during the discharge state, which are 1884, 1600, 1533, 1353, 1121, 697, 78, and 52, respectively. As the diffusion pathways within the electrode material gradually open, the diffusion resistance decreases. The Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion rate, calculated from σ<sub>ω</sub>, is displayed in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>j. From <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>j, it can be seen that during the discharge process (3.0 V-1.0 V), the diffusion rate of sodium ions is relatively low. This is because the initial intercalation of sodium ions into the electrode material forms an SEI layer, temporarily increasing the interfacial resistance and slowing the sodium ion diffusion rate. In the later stages of discharge (1.0 V-0.1 V), as the SEI layer stabilizes and the electrode material becomes fully activated, the sodium ion diffusion rate significantly increases. During the charging process (0.1 V-3.0 V), as the voltage rises, the SEI layer may thicken again, increasing the interfacial resistance and reducing the sodium ion diffusion rate. By comparison, it can be concluded that the diffusion rate of sodium ions during desodiation (charging) is higher than that during sodiation (discharging). Overall, during the initial discharge, chloride ions participate in the construction of the SEI layer, altering the surrounding environment of the ion-conducting inorganic salts and helping to form a stable interface. This promotes sodium ion intercalation and reduces resistance. The SEI formed after chloride ion interfacial modification significantly impacts the diffusion rate of sodium ions and the efficiency of electrochemical reactions during charge and discharge cycle.</div><div class="NLM_p">To gain deeper insights into how chloride-ion-regulated SEI affects SnSb alloy sodium-ion batteries, we conducted detailed characterization of the formed SEI. The Cl-SnSb/C half-cells were subjected to cycling between 3 and 0.05 V for 5 cycles under controlled current conditions. Subsequently, the SEI film was meticulously examined using SEM and TEM. After 5 cycles, SEM analysis (refer to <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.4c03240/suppl_file/ic4c03240_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S14</a>) revealed a visibly roughened surface of the Cl-SnSb/C electrode, with uneven deposition of particles. TEM imaging (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.4c03240/suppl_file/ic4c03240_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S15</a>) provided further confirmation of SEI formation, showing a uniform SEI layer covering the electrode surface, approximately 35 nm thick. Elemental mapping images indicated an even distribution of Cl and Na elements, integral components of the SEI, across the electrode surface. Typically, SEI components include organic and inorganic compounds. The XPS depth profiling was employed to elucidate the specific types of SEI compounds and their distribution within the SEI structure. The study utilized Ar<sup>+</sup> ion sputtering for 1, 2, and 5 min to create depth profiles of SEI elements, offering insights into the mechanism of reactions occurring during cycling. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a>a, prior to sputtering, the C 1s spectrum exhibits five distinct peaks at 283.6 eV (C–C), 284.4 eV (C–C or C–H), 286.9 eV (C–O–C), 288.1 eV (O = C–C), and 289.2 eV (O–C(═O)-O), corresponding to sp<sup>2</sup>C in conductive carbon, alkyl oxide sodium (RCH<sub>2</sub>ONa) groups formed from tetramer reduction, C–O–C environments, O = C–C environments, and carbonate (Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>), respectively. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref68 ref69'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref68 ref69" aria-label="reference 68 and 69">(68,69)</a> In the O 1s spectrum in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a>b, peaks at 531.0, 532.1, and 533.5 eV represent inorganic C = O (Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>), organic C = O (RCH<sub>2</sub>ONa), and organic C–O (RCH<sub>2</sub>ONa), respectively. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref70 ref71 ref72'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref70 ref71 ref72" aria-label="reference 70 to 72">(70−72)</a> Peaks observed at ≈199.3 eV for Cl 2p in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a>c and at ≈1072.3 eV for Na 1s in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a>d are attributed to Na–Cl bonds in NaCl, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref73'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref73" aria-label="reference 73">(73)</a> with an additional peak at 1071.5 eV (Na–O) indicating other sodium compounds (Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>, RCH<sub>2</sub>ONa). The SEI film comprises both organic (e.g., RCH<sub>2</sub>ONa) and inorganic (e.g., NaCl, Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>) components, predominantly organic. From <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a>a,b, after 1 min of sputtering, peaks for organic C and O weaken, while peaks for inorganic C (≈289.2 eV) and O (≈531.0 eV) slowly intensify, suggesting an increasing presence of inorganic species with sputtering time. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref74 ref75'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref74 ref75" aria-label="reference 74 and 75">(74,75)</a> <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a>f shows that initially, C = O peaks from organic components are higher compared to those from inorganic components, indicating organic species like alkyl oxide sodium are primarily distributed near the SEI surface, with inorganic species dominating internally. Analysis of Cl 2p and Na 1s spectra in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a>c,d reveals that before sputtering (0 min), peaks representing Na–Cl are stronger than those for Na–O, indicating NaCl predominates on the SEI surface, consistent with the CV curve showing NaCl formation at 1.12 V. During sputtering, Na–Cl and Na–O peaks remain sharp and intense with increasing sputtering time. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a>e demonstrates that NaCl peaks steadily increase before 2 min of sputtering and decrease after 5 min, whereas Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> peaks first decrease and then increase, aligning with C 1s spectrum results. This confirms that NaCl and Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> are the primary inorganic components within the SEI film, with chloride-introduced sodium chloride being the major constituent.</div><figure id="fig7" data-index="7" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 7</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.4c03240/asset/images/medium/ic4c03240_0007.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.4c03240/asset/images/large/ic4c03240_0007.jpeg" id="gr7" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 7. XPS depth etching analysis upon SEI components and their distribution. (a–e) Peak fitting at varying etching depth. (a) C 1s spectra, (b) O 1s spectra, (c) Cl 2p spectra, (d) Na 1s spectra. The content comparison of representative fitting peaks at varying etching depth. (e) NaCl and Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> peak, (f) C = O (inorganic) and C = O (organic) peak.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.4c03240/asset/images/large/ic4c03240_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.inorgchem.4c03240&amp;id=fig7"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i25">4.  Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">The designed anode material, Cl-SnSb/C, which consists of chlorine-ion-regulated alloy nanoparticles on a carbon substrate, exhibits excellent advantages. The bimetallic SnSb alloy nanoparticles benefit from the synergistic effects of Sn and Sb, undergoing conversion-reconstruction reactions at different reduction potentials, buffering volume changes, and reducing strain during sodiation/desodiation processes. Furthermore, the Sn and Sb alloy nanoparticles are uniformly dispersed within the carbon substrate, providing the SnSb/C with good conductivity and a microporous structure, thereby enhancing the reaction kinetics of the electrode material. The carbon substrate not only prevents particle agglomeration during cycling and alleviates volume effects caused by the electrode material but also improves the conductivity of the material. Furthermore, the surface modified with chloride ions results in the acccumulation of chlorine-containing compounds in the SEI formed during the initial cycle. Using the abundant inorganic compounds in the SEI, the Cl-SnSb/C anode maintains a specific discharge capacity of 500.4 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> after 100 cycles at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>. This strategy provides a new approach for the design of SEI film components and offers insights and directions for future researchers to explore superior sodium-ion anode materials.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes2"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i26">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.4c03240?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.4c03240</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Na adsorption sites and adsorption energy of SnSb;
